style="line-height:1.1em">\n<div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Thunder.ogg" title="File:Thunder.ogg">Rain</a></div>\n<div>\n<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="18.416326530612" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons" data-mwtitle="Thunder.ogg" data-startoffset="0" id="mwe_player_0" with <a href="/wiki/Thunder" title="Thunder">thunder</a></div>\n</div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n<tr>\n<td class="mbox-text" colspan="2" style="line-height:1.1em">\n<hr/>\n<span><i>Problems playing this file? See <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Media_help" title="Wikipedia:Media help">media help</a>.</i></span></td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n<p><b>Rain</b> is liquid <a href="/wiki/Water" title="Water">water</a> in the form of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Droplet" title="Droplet">droplets</a> that have <a href="/wiki/Condensation" title="Condensation">condensed</a> from <a href="/wiki/Atmosphere" title="Atmosphere">atmospheric</a> <a href="/wiki/Water_vapor" title="Water vapor">water vapor</a> and then <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation (meteorology)">becomes heavy enough to fall under gravity</a>. Rain is a major component of the <a href="/wiki/Water_cycle" title="Water cycle">water cycle</a> and is responsible for depositing most of the <a href="/wiki/Fresh_water" title="Fresh water">fresh water</a> on the Earth. It provides suitable conditions for many types of <a href="/wiki/Ecosystem" title="Ecosystem">ecosystems</a>, as well as water for <a href="/wiki/Hydroelectricity" title="Hydroelectricity">hydroelectric power plants</a> and crop <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a>.</p>\n<p>The major cause of rain production is moisture moving along three-dimensional zones of temperature and moisture contrasts known as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_fronts" title="Weather fronts">weather fronts</a>. If enough moisture and upward motion is present, precipitation falls from <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convective</a> clouds (those with strong upward vertical motion) such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus" title="Cumulonimbus">cumulonimbus</a> (thunder clouds) which can organize into narrow <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rainbands" title="Rainbands">rainbands</a>. In mountainous areas, heavy precipitation is possible where <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Upslope_flow" title="Upslope flow">upslope flow</a> is maximized within <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Windward" title="Windward">windward</a> sides of the <a href="/wiki/Terrain" title="Terrain">terrain</a> at elevation which forces moist air to condense and fall out as rainfall along the sides of mountains. On the leeward side of mountains, desert climates can exist due to the dry air caused by downslope flow which causes heating and drying of the <a href="/wiki/Air_mass" title="Air mass">air mass</a>. The movement of the <a href="/wiki/Monsoon_trough" title="Monsoon trough">monsoon trough</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intertropical_convergence_zone" title="Intertropical convergence zone">intertropical convergence zone</a>, brings <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">rainy seasons</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Savannah" title="Savannah">savannah</a> <a href="/wiki/Clime" title="Clime">climes</a>.</p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">urban heat island</a> effect leads to increased rainfall, both in amounts and intensity, downwind of cities. <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a> is also causing changes in the precipitation pattern globally, including wetter conditions across eastern <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a> and drier conditions in the tropics.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Antarctica is the driest continent. The globally averaged annual precipitation over land is 715\xc2\xa0mm (28.1\xc2\xa0in), but over the whole Earth it is much higher at 990\xc2\xa0mm (39\xc2\xa0in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1"><a href="#cite_note-1">[1]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Climate_classification" title="Climate classification">Climate classification</a> systems such as the <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">K\xc3\xb6ppen classification</a> system use average annual rainfall to help differentiate between differing climate regimes. Rainfall is measured using <a href="/wiki/Rain_gauge" title="Rain gauge">rain gauges</a>. Rainfall amounts can be estimated by <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a>.</p>\n<p>Rain is also known or suspected on other planets, where it may be composed of <a href="/wiki/Methane" title="Methane">methane</a>, <a href="/wiki/Neon" title="Neon">neon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a>, or even <a href="/wiki/Iron" title="Iron">iron</a> rather than water.</p>\n<p></p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc">\n<div class="toctitle">\n<h2>Contents</h2>\n</div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Formation"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Formation</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-2"><a href="#Water-saturated_air"><span impacts</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Virga"><span class="tocnumber">1.5</span> <span class="toctext">Virga</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 climate classification</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-17"><a href="#Measurement"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Measurement</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-18"><a href="#Gauges"><span class="tocnumber">4.1</span> <span class="toctext">Gauges</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Remote_sensing"><span class="tocnumber">4.2</span> <span class="toctext">Remote sensing</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Intensity"><span class="tocnumber">4.3</span> <span class="toctext">Intensity</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-21"><a href="#Return_period"><span class="tocnumber">4.4</span> <span class="toctext">Return period</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-22"><a href="#Forecasting"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Forecasting</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Impact"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Impact</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-24"><a href="#Effect_on_agriculture"><span class="tocnumber">6.1</span> <span class="toctext">Effect on agriculture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-25"><a href="#In_culture"><span class="tocnumber">6.2</span> <span class="toctext">In culture</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Global_climatology"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Global climatology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-27"><a href="#Deserts"><span desert</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-29"><a href="#Rainforests"><span class="tocnumber">7.3</span> <span class="toctext">Rainforests</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-30"><a href="#Monsoons"><span class="tocnumber">7.4</span> <span class="toctext">Monsoons</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-31"><a href="#Impact_of_the_Westerlies"><span class="tocnumber">7.5</span> <span class="toctext">Impact of the Westerlies</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-32"><a href="#Wettest_known_locations"><span class="tocnumber">7.6</span> <span class="toctext">Wettest known locations</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#Outside_Earth"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Outside Earth</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-34"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-35"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-36"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-37"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<p></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Formation">Formation</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Water-saturated_air">Water-saturated air</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:312px;">\n<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_1" media</span></span></a></div>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Averse_de_pluie.webm" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nFalling rain</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Vihm_Osula_k%C3%BCla_talivilja_p%C3%B5llul.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5184" data-file-width="3456" height="330" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Vihm_Osula_k%C3%BCla_talivilja_p%C3%B5llul.jpg/220px-Vihm_Osula_k%C3%BCla_talivilja_p%C3%B5llul.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Vihm_Osula_k%C3%BCla_talivilja_p%C3%B5llul.jpg/330px-Vihm_Osula_k%C3%BCla_talivilja_p%C3%B5llul.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/54/Vihm_Osula_k%C3%BCla_talivilja_p%C3%B5llul.jpg/440px-Vihm_Osula_k%C3%BCla_talivilja_p%C3%B5llul.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Vihm_Osula_k%C3%BCla_talivilja_p%C3%B5llul.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nRain falling on a field, in southern <a href="/wiki/Estonia" title="Estonia">Estonia</a></div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Air contains water vapor, and the amount of water in a given mass of dry air, known as the <i>mixing ratio</i>, is measured in grams of water per kilogram of dry air (g/kg).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2"><a href="#cite_note-2">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> The amount of moisture in air is also commonly reported as <a href="/wiki/Relative_humidity" title="Relative humidity">relative humidity</a>; which is the percentage of the total water vapor air can hold at a particular air temperature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> How much water vapor a parcel of air can contain before it becomes saturated (100%\xc2\xa0relative humidity) and forms into a <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a> (a group of visible and tiny water and ice particles suspended above the Earth\'s surface)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[5]</a></sup> depends on its temperature. Warmer air can contain more water vapor than cooler air before becoming saturated. Therefore, one way to saturate a parcel of air is to cool it. The <a href="/wiki/Dew_point" title="Dew point">dew point</a> is the temperature to which a parcel must be cooled in order to become saturated.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup></p>\n<p>There are four main mechanisms for cooling the air to its dew point: adiabatic cooling, conductive cooling, radiational cooling, and evaporative cooling. <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate#Dry_adiabatic_lapse_rate" title="Adiabatic lapse rate">Adiabatic cooling</a> occurs when air rises and expands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup> The air can rise due to <a href="/wiki/Convection" title="Convection">convection</a>, large-scale atmospheric motions, or a physical barrier such as a mountain (<a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">orographic lift</a>). Conductive cooling occurs when the air comes into contact with a colder surface,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[8]</a></sup> usually by being blown from one surface to another, for example from a liquid water surface to colder land. Radiational cooling occurs due to the emission of <a href="/wiki/Thermal_radiation" title="Thermal radiation">infrared radiation</a>, either by the air or by the surface underneath.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> Evaporative cooling occurs when moisture is added to the air through evaporation, which forces the air temperature to cool to its <a href="/wiki/Wet-bulb_temperature" title="Wet-bulb temperature">wet-bulb temperature</a>, or until it reaches saturation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The main ways water vapor is added to the air are: wind convergence into areas of upward motion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-convection_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-convection-11">[11]</a></sup> precipitation or virga falling from above,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> daytime heating evaporating water from the surface of oceans, water bodies or wet land,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> transpiration from plants,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup> cool or dry air moving over warmer water,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> and lifting air over mountains.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MT_16-0"><a href="#cite_note-MT-16">[16]</a></sup> Water vapor normally begins to condense on <a href="/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei" title="Cloud condensation nuclei">condensation nuclei</a> such as dust, ice, and salt in order to form clouds. Elevated portions of weather fronts (which are three-dimensional in nature)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup> force broad areas of upward motion within the Earth\'s atmosphere which form clouds decks such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Altostratus" title="Altostratus">altostratus</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cirrostratus" title="Cirrostratus">cirrostratus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DR_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-DR-18">[18]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Stratus_cloud" title="Stratus cloud">Stratus</a> is a stable cloud deck which tends to form when a cool, stable air mass is trapped underneath a warm air mass. It can also form due to the lifting of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Radiation_fog" title="Radiation fog">advection fog</a> during breezy conditions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19"><a href="#cite_note-19">[19]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Coalescence_and_fragmentation">Coalescence and fragmentation</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Raindrops_sizes.svg"><img alt="Diagram showing that very small rain drops are almost spherical in shape. As drops become larger, they become flattened on the bottom, like a hamburger bun. Very large rain drops are split into smaller ones by air resistance which makes them increasingly unstable." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="923" data-file-width="745" height="273" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Raindrops_sizes.svg/220px-Raindrops_sizes.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Raindrops_sizes.svg/330px-Raindrops_sizes.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Raindrops_sizes.svg/440px-Raindrops_sizes.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Raindrops_sizes.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nThe shape of rain drops depending upon their size</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Coalescence_(meteorology)" title="Coalescence (meteorology)">Coalescence</a> occurs when water droplets fuse to create larger water droplets. Air resistance typically causes the water droplets in a cloud to remain stationary. When air turbulence occurs, water droplets collide, producing larger droplets.</p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Black_Rain_Clouds.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1746" data-file-width="3104" height="124" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Black_Rain_Clouds.jpg/220px-Black_Rain_Clouds.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Black_Rain_Clouds.jpg/330px-Black_Rain_Clouds.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/65/Black_Rain_Clouds.jpg/440px-Black_Rain_Clouds.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Black_Rain_Clouds.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nBlack Rain Clouds</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>As these larger water droplets descend, coalescence continues, so that drops become heavy enough to overcome air resistance and fall as rain. Coalescence generally happens most often in clouds above freezing, and is also known as the warm rain process.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> In clouds below freezing, when ice crystals gain enough mass they begin to fall. This generally requires more mass than coalescence when occurring between the crystal and neighboring water droplets. This process is temperature dependent, as supercooled water droplets only exist in a cloud that is below freezing. In addition, because of the great temperature difference between cloud and ground level, these ice crystals may melt as they fall and become rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Raindrops have sizes ranging from 0.1 to 9\xc2\xa0mm (0.0039 to 0.3543\xc2\xa0in) mean diameter, above which they tend to break up. Smaller drops are called cloud droplets, and their shape is spherical. As a raindrop increases in size, its shape becomes more oblate, with its largest cross-section facing the oncoming airflow. Large rain drops become increasingly flattened on the bottom, like <a href="/wiki/Hamburger" title="Hamburger">hamburger</a> buns; very large ones are shaped like <a href="/wiki/Parachute" title="Parachute">parachutes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Villermaux_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-Villermaux-23">[23]</a></sup> Contrary to popular belief, their shape does not resemble a teardrop.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup> The biggest raindrops on Earth were recorded over <a href="/wiki/Brazil" title="Brazil">Brazil</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Marshall_Islands" title="Marshall Islands">Marshall Islands</a> in 2004\xc2\xa0\xe2\x80\x94 some of them were as large as 10\xc2\xa0mm (0.39\xc2\xa0in). The large size is explained by condensation on large <a href="/wiki/Smoke" title="Smoke">smoke</a> particles or by collisions between drops in small regions with particularly high content of liquid water.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Rain drops associated with melting hail tend to be larger than other rain drops.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup></p>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Water_Drop_on_rose_leaf.JPG"><img alt="Raindrop" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2448" data-file-width="3264" height="225" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Water_Drop_on_rose_leaf.JPG/300px-Water_Drop_on_rose_leaf.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Water_Drop_on_rose_leaf.JPG/450px-Water_Drop_on_rose_leaf.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/bd/Water_Drop_on_rose_leaf.JPG/600px-Water_Drop_on_rose_leaf.JPG 2x" width="300"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Water_Drop_on_rose_leaf.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nA raindrop on a leaf</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>Intensity and duration of rainfall are usually inversely related, i.e., high intensity storms are likely to be of short duration and low intensity storms can have a long duration.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JS_27-0"><a href="#cite_note-JS-27">[27]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Droplet_size_distribution">Droplet size distribution</span></h3>\n<p>The final droplet size distribution is an <a href="/wiki/Exponential_distribution" title="Exponential distribution">exponential distribution</a>. The number of droplets with diameter between <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>d</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle d}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="d" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e85ff03cbe0c7341af6b982e47e9f90d235c66ab" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:1.226ex; height:2.176ex;"/></span> and <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>D</mi>\n<mo>+</mo>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle D+dD}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="D+dD" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c543d9fd275f8035889bc85aff288ac43c43c451" style="vertical-align: -0.505ex; width:7.947ex; height:2.343ex;"/></span> per unit volume of space is <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">(</mo>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mo stretchy="false">)</mo>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<msub>\n<mi>n</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mn>0</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msub>\n<msup>\n<mi>e</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>\xe2\x88\x92<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>/</mo>\n</mrow>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">\xe2\x9f\xa8<!-- \xe2\x9f\xa8 --></mo>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">\xe2\x9f\xa9<!-- \xe2\x9f\xa9 --></mo>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<mi>D</mi>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle n(d)=n_{0}e^{-d/\\langle d\\rangle }dD}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="n(d)=n_{0}e^{{-d/\\langle d\\rangle }}dD" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c1fa5db89ff1ed652a70ac8f4e0c730ff8e474b3" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:19.668ex; height:3.343ex;"/></span>. This is commonly referred to as the Marshall\xe2\x80\x93Palmer law after the researchers who first characterized it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Villermaux_23-1"><a href="#cite_note-Villermaux-23">[23]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup> The parameters are somewhat temperature-dependent,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup> and the slope also scales with the rate of rainfall <span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">\xe2\x9f\xa8<!-- \xe2\x9f\xa8 --></mo>\n<mi>d</mi>\n<msup>\n<mo fence="false" stretchy="false">\xe2\x9f\xa9<!-- \xe2\x9f\xa9 --></mo>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>\xe2\x88\x92<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n<mn>1</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mn>41</mn>\n<msup>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mo>\xe2\x88\x92<!-- \xe2\x88\x92 --></mo>\n<mn>0.21</mn>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle \\langle d\\rangle ^{-1}=41R^{-0.21}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="\\langle d\\rangle ^{{-1}}=41R^{{-0.21}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/d9849e9ae5a18e5fcb558d35626c46a2370fac35" style="vertical-align: -0.838ex; width:17.104ex; height:3.176ex;"/></span> (d in centimeters and R in millimetres per hour).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Villermaux_23-2"><a href="#cite_note-Villermaux-23">[23]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Deviations can occur for small droplets and during different rainfall conditions. The distribution tends to fit averaged rainfall, while instantaneous size spectra often deviate and have been modeled as <a href="/wiki/Gamma_distribution" title="Gamma distribution">gamma distributions</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The distribution has an upper limit due to droplet fragmentation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Villermaux_23-3"><a href="#cite_note-Villermaux-23">[23]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Raindrop_impacts">Raindrop impacts</span></h3>\n<p>Raindrops impact at their <a href="/wiki/Terminal_velocity" title="Terminal velocity">terminal velocity</a>, which is greater for larger drops due to their larger mass to drag ratio. At sea level and without wind, 0.5\xc2\xa0mm (0.020\xc2\xa0in) <a href="/wiki/Drizzle" title="Drizzle">drizzle</a> impacts at 2\xc2\xa0m/s (6.6\xc2\xa0ft/s) or 7.2\xc2\xa0km/h (4.5\xc2\xa0mph), while large 5\xc2\xa0mm (0.20\xc2\xa0in) drops impact at around 9\xc2\xa0m/s (30\xc2\xa0ft/s) or 32\xc2\xa0km/h (20\xc2\xa0mph).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Rain falling on loosely packed material such as newly fallen ash can produce dimples that can be fossilized.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup> The air density dependence of the maximum raindrop diameter together with fossil raindrop imprints has been used to constrain the density of the air 2.7 billion years ago.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Droplet#Sound" title="Droplet">sound of raindrops</a> hitting water is caused by bubbles of air <a href="/wiki/Minnaert_resonance" title="Minnaert resonance">oscillating underwater</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The <a href="/wiki/METAR" title="METAR">METAR</a> code for rain is RA, while the coding for rain showers is SHRA.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-METAR_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-METAR-37">[37]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Virga">Virga</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Virga" title="Virga">Virga</a></div>\n<p>In certain conditions precipitation may fall from a cloud but then evaporate or <a class="mw-disambig" href="/wiki/Sublimation" title="Sublimation">sublime</a> before reaching the ground. This is termed <a href="/wiki/Virga" title="Virga">virga</a> and is more often seen in hot and dry climates.</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Causes">Causes</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Frontal_activity">Frontal activity</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weather_fronts" title="Weather fronts">Weather fronts</a></div>\n<p>Stratiform (a broad shield of precipitation with a relatively similar intensity) and dynamic precipitation (convective precipitation which is showery in nature with large changes in intensity over short distances) occur as a consequence of slow ascent of air in <a href="/wiki/Synoptic_scale_meteorology" title="Synoptic scale meteorology">synoptic systems</a> (on the order of cm/s), such as in the vicinity of <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold fronts</a> and near and poleward of surface <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">warm fronts</a>. Similar ascent is seen around <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">tropical cyclones</a> outside the <a href="/wiki/Eye_(cyclone)" title="Eye (cyclone)">eyewall</a>, and in comma-head precipitation patterns around <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mid-latitude_cyclone" title="Mid-latitude cyclone">mid-latitude cyclones</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geerts_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-Geerts-38">[38]</a></sup> A wide variety of weather can be found along an occluded front, with thunderstorms possible, but usually their passage is associated with a drying of the air mass. Occluded fronts usually form around mature low-pressure areas.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-autogenerated3_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-autogenerated3-39">[39]</a></sup> What separates rainfall from other precipitation types, such as <a href="/wiki/Ice_pellets" title="Ice pellets">ice pellets</a> and <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">snow</a>, is the presence of a thick layer of air aloft which is above the melting point of water, which melts the frozen precipitation well before it reaches the ground. If there is a shallow near surface layer that is below freezing, freezing rain (rain which freezes on contact with surfaces in subfreezing environments) will result.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-40"><a href="#cite_note-40">[40]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">Hail</a> becomes an increasingly infrequent occurrence when the freezing level within the atmosphere exceeds 3,400\xc2\xa0m (11,000\xc2\xa0ft) above ground level.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-mesoanal_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-mesoanal-41">[41]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Convection">Convection</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Konvektionsregen.jpg"><img alt="Diagram showing that as moist air becomes heated more than its surroundings, it moves upward, resulting in brief rain showers." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="436" data-file-width="610" height="157" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Konvektionsregen.jpg/220px-Konvektionsregen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Konvektionsregen.jpg/330px-Konvektionsregen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/57/Konvektionsregen.jpg/440px-Konvektionsregen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Konvektionsregen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nConvective precipitation</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Steigungsregen.jpg"><img alt="Diagram showing how moist air over the ocean rises and flows over the land, causing cooling and rain as it hits mountain ridges." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="454" data-file-width="612" height="163" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Steigungsregen.jpg/220px-Steigungsregen.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Steigungsregen.jpg/330px-Steigungsregen.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/88/Steigungsregen.jpg/440px-Steigungsregen.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Steigungsregen.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nOrographic precipitation</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Convection_rain" title="Convection rain">Convective rain</a>, or showery precipitation, occurs from convective clouds (e.g., <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulonimbus" title="Cumulonimbus">cumulonimbus</a> or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cumulus_congestus" title="Cumulus congestus">cumulus congestus</a>). It falls as showers with rapidly changing intensity. Convective precipitation falls over a certain area for a relatively short time, as convective clouds have limited horizontal extent. Most precipitation in the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> appears to be convective; however, it has been suggested that stratiform precipitation also occurs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Geerts_38-1"><a href="#cite_note-Geerts-38">[38]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[42]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Graupel" title="Graupel">Graupel</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a> indicate convection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[43]</a></sup> In mid-latitudes, convective precipitation is intermittent and often associated with baroclinic boundaries such as <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold fronts</a>, <a href="/wiki/Squall_line" title="Squall line">squall lines</a>, and warm fronts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-44"><a href="#cite_note-44">[44]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Orographic_effects">Orographic effects</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main articles: <a href="/wiki/Orographic_lift" title="Orographic lift">Orographic lift</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Precipitation_types_(meteorology)" title="Precipitation types (meteorology)">Precipitation types (meteorology)</a>, and <a href="/wiki/United_States_rainfall_climatology" title="United States rainfall climatology">United States rainfall climatology</a></div>\n<p>Orographic precipitation occurs on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Windward" title="Windward">windward</a> side of mountains and is caused by the rising air motion of a large-scale flow of moist air across the mountain ridge, resulting in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adiabatic_lapse_rate" title="Adiabatic lapse rate">adiabatic</a> cooling and condensation. In mountainous parts of the world subjected to relatively consistent winds (for example, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trade_wind" title="Trade wind">trade winds</a>), a more moist <a href="/wiki/Climate" title="Climate">climate</a> usually prevails on the windward side of a mountain than on the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Leeward" title="Leeward">leeward</a> or downwind side. Moisture is removed by orographic lift, leaving drier air (see <a href="/wiki/Katabatic_wind" title="Katabatic wind">katabatic wind</a>) on the descending and generally warming, leeward side where a <a href="/wiki/Rain_shadow" title="Rain shadow">rain shadow</a> is observed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-MT_16-1"><a href="#cite_note-MT-16">[16]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Hawaii" title="Hawaii">Hawaii</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Wai%CA%BBale%CA%BBale" title="Mount Wai\xca\xbbale\xca\xbbale">Mount Wai\xca\xbbale\xca\xbbale</a>, on the island of Kauai, is notable for its extreme rainfall, as it has the second highest average annual rainfall on Earth, with 12,000\xc2\xa0mm (460\xc2\xa0in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-45"><a href="#cite_note-45">[45]</a></sup> Systems known as <a href="/wiki/Kona_storm" title="Kona storm">Kona storms</a> affect the state with heavy rains between October and April.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-BIRCH_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-BIRCH-46">[46]</a></sup> Local climates vary considerably on each island due to their topography, divisible into windward (<i>Ko<span class="unicode">\xca\xbb</span>olau</i>) and leeward (<i>Kona</i>) regions based upon location relative to the higher mountains. Windward sides face the east to northeast <a href="/wiki/Trade_winds" title="Trade winds">trade winds</a> and receive much more rainfall; leeward sides are drier and sunnier, with less rain and less cloud cover.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-47"><a href="#cite_note-47">[47]</a></sup></p>\n<p>In South America, the <a href="/wiki/Andes" title="Andes">Andes</a> mountain range blocks <a href="/wiki/Pacific_Ocean" title="Pacific Ocean">Pacific</a> moisture that arrives in that continent, resulting in a desertlike climate just downwind across western Argentina.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Andes_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-Andes-48">[48]</a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Sierra_Nevada_(U.S.)" title="Sierra Nevada (U.S.)">Sierra Nevada</a> range creates the same effect in North America forming the <a href="/wiki/Great_Basin" title="Great Basin">Great Basin</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mojave_Desert" title="Mojave Desert">Mojave Deserts</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-49"><a href="#cite_note-49">[49]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-50"><a href="#cite_note-50">[50]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Within_the_tropics">Within the tropics</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cairns_climate.svg"><img alt="Chart showing an Australian city with as much as 450 mm of rain in the winter months and less than 50 mm in the summer." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="800" height="188" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e5/Cairns_climate.svg/250px-Cairns_climate.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e5/Cairns_climate.svg/375px-Cairns_climate.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/e/e5/Cairns_climate.svg/500px-Cairns_climate.svg.png 2x" width="250"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cairns_climate.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nRainfall distribution by month in <a href="/wiki/Cairns" title="Cairns">Cairns</a> showing the extent of the wet season at that location</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">Monsoon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">Tropical cyclone</a></div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Wet_season" title="Wet season">Wet season</a></div>\n<p>The wet, or rainy, season is the time of year, covering one or more months, when most of the average annual rainfall in a region falls.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-51"><a href="#cite_note-51">[51]</a></sup> The term <i>green season</i> is also sometimes used as a <a href="/wiki/Euphemism" title="Euphemism">euphemism</a> by tourist authorities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-52"><a href="#cite_note-52">[52]</a></sup> Areas with wet seasons are dispersed across portions of the <a href="/wiki/Tropics" title="Tropics">tropics</a> and <a href="/wiki/Subtropics" title="Subtropics">subtropics</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-53"><a href="#cite_note-53">[53]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Savanna" title="Savanna">Savanna</a> climates and areas with <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a> regimes have wet summers and dry winters. Tropical rainforests technically do not have dry or wet seasons, since their rainfall is equally distributed through the year.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hyde_54-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hyde-54">[54]</a></sup> Some areas with pronounced rainy seasons will see a break in rainfall mid-season when the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Intertropical_convergence_zone" title="Intertropical convergence zone">intertropical convergence zone</a> or <a href="/wiki/Monsoon_trough" title="Monsoon trough">monsoon trough</a> move poleward of their location during the middle of the warm season.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-JS_27-1"><a href="#cite_note-JS-27">[27]</a></sup> When the wet season occurs during the warm season, or <a href="/wiki/Summer" title="Summer">summer</a>, rain falls mainly during the late afternoon and early evening hours. The wet season is a time when <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_quality" title="Air quality">air quality</a> improves,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[55]</a></sup> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freshwater" title="Freshwater">freshwater</a> quality improves,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-56"><a href="#cite_note-56">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[57]</a></sup> and vegetation grows significantly.</p>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">Tropical cyclones</a>, a source of very heavy rainfall, consist of large air masses several hundred miles across with low pressure at the centre and with winds blowing inward towards the centre in either a clockwise direction (southern hemisphere) or counter clockwise (northern hemisphere).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-58"><a href="#cite_note-58">[58]</a></sup> Although <a href="/wiki/Cyclone" title="Cyclone">cyclones</a> can take an enormous toll in lives and personal property, they may be important factors in the precipitation regimes of places they impact, as they may bring much-needed precipitation to otherwise dry regions.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2005_EPac_outlook_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-2005_EPac_outlook-59">[59]</a></sup> Areas in their path can receive a year\'s worth of rainfall from a tropical cyclone passage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-60"><a href="#cite_note-60">[60]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Human_influence">Human influence</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Atlanta_thermal.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="379" data-file-width="540" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Atlanta_thermal.jpg/200px-Atlanta_thermal.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Atlanta_thermal.jpg/300px-Atlanta_thermal.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0a/Atlanta_thermal.jpg/400px-Atlanta_thermal.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Atlanta_thermal.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nImage of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atlanta,_Georgia" title="Atlanta, Georgia">Atlanta, Georgia</a> showing temperature distribution, with blue showing cool temperatures, red warm, and hot areas appearing white.</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Global_Warming_Map.jpg"><img alt="World map of temperature distribution shows the northern hemisphere was warmer than the southern hemisphere during the periods compared." class="thumbimage" data-file-height="596" data-file-width="800" height="149" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Global_Warming_Map.jpg/200px-Global_Warming_Map.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Global_Warming_Map.jpg/300px-Global_Warming_Map.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8c/Global_Warming_Map.jpg/400px-Global_Warming_Map.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Global_Warming_Map.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nMean surface temperature anomalies during the period 1999 to 2008 with respect to the average temperatures from 1940 to 1980</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" title="Global warming">Global warming</a> and <a href="/wiki/Urban_heat_island" title="Urban heat island">Urban heat island</a></div>\n<p>The fine particulate matter produced by car exhaust and other human sources of pollution forms <a href="/wiki/Cloud_condensation_nuclei" title="Cloud condensation nuclei">cloud condensation nuclei</a>, leads to the production of clouds and increases the likelihood of rain. As commuters and commercial traffic cause pollution to build up over the course of the week, the likelihood of rain increases: it peaks by Saturday, after five days of weekday pollution has been built up. In heavily populated areas that are near the coast, such as the United States\' <a href="/wiki/East_Coast_of_the_United_States" title="East Coast of the United States">Eastern Seaboard</a>, the effect can be dramatic: there is a 22% higher chance of rain on Saturdays than on Mondays.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-61"><a href="#cite_note-61">[61]</a></sup> The urban heat island effect warms cities 0.6 to 5.6\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0C (1.1 to 10.1\xc2\xa0\xc2\xb0F) above surrounding suburbs and rural areas. This extra heat leads to greater upward motion, which can induce additional shower and thunderstorm activity. Rainfall rates downwind of cities are increased between 48% and 116%. Partly as a result of this warming, monthly rainfall is about 28% greater between 32 to 64\xc2\xa0km (20 to 40\xc2\xa0mi) downwind of cities, compared with upwind.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[62]</a></sup> Some cities induce a total precipitation increase of 51%.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[63]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Increasing temperatures tend to increase evaporation which can lead to more precipitation. Precipitation generally increased over land north of 30\xc2\xb0N from 1900 through 2005 but has declined over the tropics since the 1970s. Globally there has been no statistically significant overall trend in precipitation over the past century, although trends have varied widely by region and over time. Eastern portions of North and South America, northern Europe, and northern and central Asia have become wetter. The Sahel, the Mediterranean, southern Africa and parts of southern Asia have become drier. There has been an increase in the number of heavy precipitation events over many areas during the past century, as well as an increase since the 1970s in the prevalence of droughts\xe2\x80\x94especially in the tropics and subtropics. Changes in precipitation and evaporation over the oceans are suggested by the decreased salinity of mid- and high-latitude waters (implying more precipitation), along with increased salinity in lower latitudes (implying less precipitation and/or more evaporation). Over the contiguous United States, total annual precipitation increased at an average rate of 6.1\xc2\xa0percent since 1900, with the greatest increases within the East North Central climate region (11.6 percent per century) and the South (11.1\xc2\xa0percent). Hawaii was the only region to show a decrease (\xe2\x88\x929.25\xc2\xa0percent).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[64]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Analysis of 65 years of United States of America rainfall records show the lower 48 states have an increase in <a class="new" href="/w/index.php?title=Heavy_downpour&amp;action=edit&amp;redlink=1" title="Heavy downpour (page does not exist)">heavy downpour</a>s since 1950. The largest increases are in the Northeast and Midwest, which in the past decade, have seen 31 and 16 percent more heavy downpours compared to the 1950s. Rhode Island is the state with the largest increase, 104%. McAllen, Texas is the city with the largest increase, 700%. Heavy downpour in the analysis are the days where total precipitation exceeded the top 1 percent of all rain and snow days during the years 1950-2014<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-65"><a href="#cite_note-65">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-66"><a href="#cite_note-66">[66]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The most successful attempts at influencing <a href="/wiki/Weather" title="Weather">weather</a> involve <a href="/wiki/Cloud_seeding" title="Cloud seeding">cloud seeding</a>, which include techniques used to increase <a href="/wiki/Snow" title="Snow">winter precipitation</a> over mountains and suppress <a href="/wiki/Hail" title="Hail">hail</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-AMSmod_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-AMSmod-67">[67]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Characteristics">Characteristics</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Patterns">Patterns</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="864" data-file-width="1196" height="144" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg/200px-Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg/300px-Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0c/Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg/400px-Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Sturmfront_auf_Doppler-Radar-Schirm.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nBand of thunderstorms seen on a <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a> display</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Rainband" title="Rainband">Rainband</a></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Rainband" title="Rainband">Rainbands</a> are <a href="/wiki/Cloud" title="Cloud">cloud</a> and precipitation areas which are significantly elongated. Rainbands can be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Stratiform" title="Stratiform">stratiform</a> or <a href="/wiki/Atmospheric_convection" title="Atmospheric convection">convective</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[68]</a></sup> and are generated by differences in temperature. When noted on <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">weather radar</a> imagery, this precipitation elongation is referred to as banded structure.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-69"><a href="#cite_note-69">[69]</a></sup> Rainbands in advance of warm <a href="/wiki/Occluded_front" title="Occluded front">occluded fronts</a> and <a href="/wiki/Warm_front" title="Warm front">warm fronts</a> are associated with weak upward motion,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[70]</a></sup> and tend to be wide and stratiform in nature.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Rainbands spawned near and ahead of <a href="/wiki/Cold_front" title="Cold front">cold fronts</a> can be <a href="/wiki/Squall_line" title="Squall line">squall lines</a> which are able to produce <a href="/wiki/Tornado" title="Tornado">tornadoes</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup> Rainbands associated with cold fronts can be warped by mountain barriers perpendicular to the front\'s orientation due to the formation of a low-level <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Barrier_jet" title="Barrier jet">barrier jet</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup> Bands of thunderstorms can form with <a href="/wiki/Sea_breeze" title="Sea breeze">sea breeze</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Land_breeze" title="Land breeze">land breeze</a> boundaries, if enough moisture is present. If sea breeze rainbands become active enough just ahead of a cold front, they can mask the location of the cold front itself.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Once a cyclone occludes, a <b>tr</b>ough <b>o</b>f <b>w</b>arm air <b>al</b>oft, or "<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trowal" title="Trowal">trowal</a>" for short, will be caused by strong southerly winds on its eastern periphery rotating aloft around its northeast, and ultimately northwestern, periphery (also known as the warm conveyor belt), forcing a surface trough to continue into the cold sector on a similar curve to the occluded front. The trowal creates the portion of an occluded cyclone known as its comma head, due to the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Comma_(punctuation)" title="Comma (punctuation)">comma</a>-like shape of the mid-tropospheric cloudiness that accompanies the feature. It can also be the focus of locally heavy precipitation, with thunderstorms possible if the atmosphere along the trowal is unstable enough for convection.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-TROW_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-TROW-75">[75]</a></sup> Banding within the comma head precipitation pattern of an <a href="/wiki/Extratropical_cyclone" title="Extratropical cyclone">extratropical cyclone</a> can yield significant amounts of rain.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup> Behind extratropical cyclones during fall and winter, rainbands can form downwind of relative warm bodies of water such as the <a href="/wiki/Great_Lakes" title="Great Lakes">Great Lakes</a>. Downwind of islands, bands of showers and thunderstorms can develop due to low level wind convergence downwind of the island edges. Offshore <a href="/wiki/California" title="California">California</a>, this has been noted in the wake of cold fronts.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></p>\n<p>Rainbands within tropical cyclones are curved in orientation. Tropical cyclone rainbands contain showers and thunderstorms that, together with the eyewall and the eye, constitute a <a href="/wiki/Tropical_cyclone" title="Tropical cyclone">hurricane or tropical storm</a>. The extent of rainbands around a tropical cyclone can help determine the cyclone\'s intensity.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-ODT_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-ODT-78">[78]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Acidity">Acidity</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Origins_of_acid_rain.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="303" data-file-width="450" height="148" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Origins_of_acid_rain.svg/220px-Origins_of_acid_rain.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Origins_of_acid_rain.svg/330px-Origins_of_acid_rain.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/Origins_of_acid_rain.svg/440px-Origins_of_acid_rain.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Origins_of_acid_rain.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nSources of acid rain</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Acid_rain" title="Acid rain">Acid rain</a></div>\n<p>The phrase <i>acid rain</i> was first used by Scottish chemist Robert Augus Smith in 1852.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup> The pH of rain varies, especially due to its origin. On America\'s East Coast, rain that is derived from the Atlantic Ocean typically has a pH of 5.0-5.6; rain that comes across the continental from the west has a pH of 3.8-4.8; and local thunderstorms can have a pH as low as 2.0.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[80]</a></sup> Rain becomes acidic primarily due to the presence of two strong acids, <a href="/wiki/Sulfuric_acid" title="Sulfuric acid">sulfuric acid</a> (H<sub>2</sub>SO<sub>4</sub>) and <a href="/wiki/Nitric_acid" title="Nitric acid">nitric acid</a> (HNO<sub>3</sub>). Sulfuric acid is derived from natural sources such as volcanoes, and wetlands (sulfate reducing bacteria); and anthropogenic sources such as the combustion of fossil fuels, and mining where H<sub>2</sub>S is present. Nitric acid is produced by natural sources such as lightning, soil bacteria, and natural fires; while also produced anthropogenically by the combustion of fossil fuels and from power plants. In the past 20 years the concentrations of nitric and sulfuric acid has decreased in presence of rainwater, which may be due to the significant increase in ammonium (most likely as ammonia from livestock production), which acts as a <a href="/wiki/Buffer_solution" title="Buffer solution">buffer</a> in acid rain and raises the pH.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span id="K\xc3\xb6ppen_climate_classification"></span><span class="mw-headline" id="K.C3.B6ppen_climate_classification">K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:World_Koppen_Classification_(with_authors).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2000" data-file-width="3000" height="267" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/World_Koppen_Classification_%28with_authors%29.svg/400px-World_Koppen_Classification_%28with_authors%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/World_Koppen_Classification_%28with_authors%29.svg/600px-World_Koppen_Classification_%28with_authors%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/60/World_Koppen_Classification_%28with_authors%29.svg/800px-World_Koppen_Classification_%28with_authors%29.svg.png 2x" width="400"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:World_Koppen_Classification_(with_authors).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nUpdated K\xc3\xb6ppen-Geiger climate map<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup>\n<table>\n<tr valign="top">\n<td>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #0000FE; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Equatorial_climate" title="Equatorial climate">Af</a></div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #C600C7; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">Dsb</a></div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #966495; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Continental_climate" title="Continental climate">Dsd</a></div>\n</td>\n<td width="5"></td>\n<td>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #ABB1FF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">Dwa</a></div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #5A77DB; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">Dwb</a></div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #4C51B5; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">Dwc</a></div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #320087; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">Dwd</a></div>\n</td>\n<td width="5"></td>\n<td>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00FFFF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">Dfa</a></div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #38C7FF; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Humid_continental_climate" title="Humid continental climate">Dfb</a></div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #007E7D; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">Dfc</a></div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #00455E; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">Dfd</a></div>\n</td>\n<td width="5"></td>\n<td>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #B2B2B2; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tundra_climate" title="Tundra climate">ET</a></div>\n<div class="legend" style="-webkit-column-break-inside: avoid;page-break-inside: avoid;break-inside: avoid-column"><span class="legend-color" style="display:inline-block; width:1.5em; height:1.5em; margin:1px 0; border:1px solid black; background-color: #686868; color:black; font-size:100%; text-align:center;">\xc2\xa0</span>\xc2\xa0<a href="/wiki/Ice_cap_climate" title="Ice cap climate">EF</a></div>\n</td>\n</tr>\n</table>\n</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/K%C3%B6ppen_climate_classification" title="K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification">K\xc3\xb6ppen climate classification</a></div>\n<p>The K\xc3\xb6ppen classification depends on average monthly values of temperature and precipitation. The most commonly used form of the K\xc3\xb6ppen classification has five primary types labeled A through E. Specifically, the primary types are A, tropical; B, dry; C, mild mid-latitude; D, cold mid-latitude; and E, polar. The five primary classifications can be further divided into secondary classifications such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rain_forest" title="Rain forest">rain forest</a>, <a href="/wiki/Monsoon" title="Monsoon">monsoon</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical_savanna" title="Tropical savanna">tropical savanna</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humid_subtropical" title="Humid subtropical">humid subtropical</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humid_continental" title="Humid continental">humid continental</a>, <a href="/wiki/Oceanic_climate" title="Oceanic climate">oceanic climate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_climate" title="Mediterranean climate">Mediterranean climate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Steppe" title="Steppe">steppe</a>, <a href="/wiki/Subarctic_climate" title="Subarctic climate">subarctic climate</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tundra" title="Tundra">tundra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Polar_ice_cap" title="Polar ice cap">polar ice cap</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Desert" title="Desert">desert</a>.</p>\n<p>Rain forests are characterized by high rainfall, with definitions setting minimum normal annual rainfall between 1,750 and 2,000\xc2\xa0mm (69 and 79\xc2\xa0in).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> A tropical savanna is a <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a> <a href="/wiki/Biome" title="Biome">biome</a> located in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-arid" title="Semi-arid">semi-arid</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Humid" title="Humid">semi-humid</a> climate regions of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Subtropical" title="Subtropical">subtropical</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tropical" title="Tropical">tropical</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latitudes" title="Latitudes">latitudes</a>, with rainfall between 750 and 1,270\xc2\xa0mm (30 and 50\xc2\xa0in) a year. They are widespread on <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, and are also found in <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a>, the northern parts of <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a>, <a href="/wiki/Malaysia" title="Malaysia">Malaysia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-SAVWOOD_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-SAVWOOD-84">[84]</a></sup> The humid subtropical climate zone is where winter rainfall is associated with large <a href="/wiki/Storm" title="Storm">storms</a> that the <a href="/wiki/Westerlies" title="Westerlies">westerlies</a> steer from west to east. Most summer rainfall occurs during thunderstorms and from occasional tropical cyclones.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Humid subtropical climates lie on the east side continents, roughly between <a href="/wiki/Latitude" title="Latitude">latitudes</a> 20\xc2\xb0 and 40\xc2\xb0 degrees away from the equator.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup></p>\n<p>An oceanic (or maritime) climate is typically found along the west coasts at the middle latitudes of all the world\'s continents, bordering cool oceans, as well as southeastern <a href="/wiki/Australia" title="Australia">Australia</a>, and is accompanied by plentiful precipitation year-round.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> The Mediterranean climate regime resembles the climate of the lands in the <a href="/wiki/Mediterranean_Basin" title="Mediterranean Basin">Mediterranean Basin</a>, parts of western <a href="/wiki/North_America" title="North America">North America</a>, parts of <a href="/wiki/Western_Australia" title="Western Australia">Western</a> and <a href="/wiki/South_Australia" title="South Australia">South Australia</a>, in southwestern <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> and in parts of central <a href="/wiki/Chile" title="Chile">Chile</a>. The climate is characterized by hot, dry summers and cool, wet winters.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> A steppe is a dry <a href="/wiki/Grassland" title="Grassland">grassland</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup> Subarctic climates are cold with continuous <a href="/wiki/Permafrost" title="Permafrost">permafrost</a> and little precipitation.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-subritter_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-subritter-90">[90]</a></sup></p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Measurement">Measurement</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Gauges">Gauges</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:127px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1156" data-file-width="544" height="266" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg/125px-250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg/188px-250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ca/250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg/250px-250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg 2x" width="125"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:250mm_Rain_Gauge.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nStandard rain gauge</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Rain_gauge" title="Rain gauge">Rain gauge</a>, <a href="/wiki/Disdrometer" title="Disdrometer">Disdrometer</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Snow_gauge" title="Snow gauge">Snow gauge</a></div>\n<p>Rain is measured in units of length per unit time, typically in millimeters per hour,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> or in countries where <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">imperial units</a> are more common, inches per hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> The "length", or more accurately, "depth" being measured is the depth of rain water that would accumulate on a flat, horizontal and impermeable surface during a given amount of time, typically an hour.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup> One millimeter of rainfall is the equivalent of one liter of water per square meter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup></p>\n<p>The standard way of measuring rainfall or snowfall is the standard rain gauge, which can be found in 100-mm (4-in) plastic and 200-mm (8-in) metal varieties.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup> The inner cylinder is filled by 25\xc2\xa0mm (0.98\xc2\xa0in) of rain, with overflow flowing into the outer cylinder. Plastic gauges have markings on the inner cylinder down to 0.25\xc2\xa0mm (0.0098\xc2\xa0in) resolution, while metal gauges require use of a stick designed with the appropriate 0.25\xc2\xa0mm (0.0098\xc2\xa0in) markings. After the inner cylinder is filled, the amount inside it is discarded, then filled with the remaining rainfall in the outer cylinder until all the fluid in the outer cylinder is gone, adding to the overall total until the outer cylinder is empty.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup> Other types of gauges include the popular wedge gauge (the cheapest rain gauge and most fragile), the tipping bucket rain gauge, and the weighing rain gauge.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> For those looking to measure rainfall the most inexpensively, a can that is cylindrical with straight sides will act as a rain gauge if left out in the open, but its accuracy will depend on what ruler is used to measure the rain with. Any of the above rain gauges can be made at home, with enough know-how.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup></p>\n<p>When a precipitation measurement is made, various networks exist across the United States and elsewhere where rainfall measurements can be submitted through the Internet, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Community_Collaborative_Rain,_Hail_and_Snow_network" title="Community Collaborative Rain, Hail and Snow network">CoCoRAHS</a> or GLOBE.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup> If a network is not available in the area where one lives, the nearest local weather or met office will likely be interested in the measurement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Remote_sensing">Remote sensing</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Weather_radar" title="Weather radar">Weather radar</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Radar-accumulations_eng.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="624" data-file-width="800" height="156" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radar-accumulations_eng.png/200px-Radar-accumulations_eng.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radar-accumulations_eng.png/300px-Radar-accumulations_eng.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/a/a2/Radar-accumulations_eng.png/400px-Radar-accumulations_eng.png 2x" width="200"/></a>\n<div class="thumbcaption">\n<div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Radar-accumulations_eng.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>\nTwenty-four-hour rainfall accumulation on the Val d\'Ir\xc3\xa8ne radar in Eastern Canada. Zones without data in the east and southwest are caused by beam blocking from mountains. (Source: Environment Canada)</div>\n</div>\n</div>\n<p>One of the main uses of weather radar is to be able to assess the amount of precipitations fallen over large basins for <a href="/wiki/Hydrology" title="Hydrology">hydrological</a> purposes.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> For instance, river <a href="/wiki/Flood_control" title="Flood control">flood control</a>, sewer management and dam construction are all areas where planners use rainfall accumulation data. Radar-derived rainfall estimates compliment surface station data which can be used for calibration. To produce radar accumulations, rain rates over a point are estimated by using the value of reflectivity data at individual grid points. A radar equation is then used, which is,</p>\n<dl>\n<dd><span class="mwe-math-element"><span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;"><math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n<semantics>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n<mi>Z</mi>\n<mo>=</mo>\n<mi>A</mi>\n<msup>\n<mi>R</mi>\n<mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n<mi>b</mi>\n</mrow>\n</msup>\n</mstyle>\n</mrow>\n<annotation encoding="application/x-tex">{\\displaystyle Z=AR^{b}}</annotation>\n</semantics>\n</math></span><img alt="Z=AR^{b}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/ce4616aa3ff4d1364cd397e8f34d72415fab35bf" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:9.273ex; height:2.676ex;"/></span>,</dd>\n</dl>\n<p>where Z represents the radar reflectivity, R represents the rainfall rate, and A and b are constants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup> Satellite derived rainfall estimates use passive <a href="/wiki/Microwave" title="Microwave">microwave</a> instruments aboard <a href="/wiki/Polar_orbit" title="Polar orbit">polar orbiting</a> as well as <a href="/wiki/Geostationary_orbit" title="Geostationary orbit">geostationary</a> <a href="/wiki/Weather_satellite" title="Weather satellite">weather satellites</a> to indirectly measure rainfall rates.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> If one wants an accumulated rainfall over a time period, one has to add up all the accumulations from each grid box within the images during that time.</p>\n<div class="thumb tmulti tleft">\n<div class="thumbinner" style="width:608px;max-width:608px">\n<div class="tsingle" style="float:left;margin:1px;width:302px;max-width:302px">\n<div class="thumbimage">\n<div class="PopUpMediaTransform" id="mwe_player_3" style="clear:both;"></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Intensity">Intensity</span></h3>\n<table class="mbox-small" role="presentation" style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;">\n<tr>\n<td class="mbox-image">\n<div class="center">\n<div class="floatnone"><img alt="" data-file-height="160" data-file-width="160" height="50" style="line-height:1.1em">\n<div class="haudio">\n<div style="padding:4px 0"><a href="/wiki/File:Heavy_rain_in_Glenshaw,_PA.ogg" title="File:Heavy rain in Glenshaw, PA.ogg">Heavy rain in Glenshaw, Pennsylvania</a></div>\n<div>\n<div class="mediaContainer" style="width:220px"><audio class="kskin" controls="" data-durationhint="336.8" data-mwprovider="wikimediacommons"
